        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912"><meta property="og:title" content="Microporous Carbon Nanospheres with Fast Sodium Storage Capability Enabled by Dominant Capacitive Behavior"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/acs.langmuir.2c00912.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Hard carbon is considered one of the most promising anode candidates for sodium ion batteries but suffers from a moderate rate performance. Here, we design microporous carbon nanospheres using a novel hybrid monomer that simultaneously involves an organic moiety and an inorganic moiety as the starting unit. The inorganic moiety forms a continuous network, which serves as a 3D scaffold and a nanometer-scale template, then supports the off-collapse of the carbon skeleton and creates a well-developed microporous structure. In addition, the graphite microcrystal structure can be tailored by adjusting the heating treatment temperatures. The electrochemical study demonstrates that the microporous carbon nanospheres show dominant capacitive sodium storage behavior, thus presenting an outstanding rate performance. Even if a very high current density of 10 A g–1 is applied, the hard carbon anode can deliver a large capacity of 127 mAh g–1 with a considerable plateau capacity of 53 mAh g–1, which has rarely been obtained in previous publications. Besides, the carbon anode has a good cycling stability, and the capacity reached 210 mAh g–1 after 1000 cycles with a current density of 1 A g–1, showing no dramatic capacity loss."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/acs.langmuir.2c00912.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6e2134e88cd95-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e2134e88cd95-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'a07063c6-d689-4ad5-8ab0-5090ba0bd1a8'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e2134e88cd95-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'langmuir';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/langmuir/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/langmuir/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="90f6e2134e88cd95-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "langd5";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Microporous Carbon Nanospheres with Fast Sodium Storage Capability Enabled by Dominant Capacitive Behavior", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Microporous Carbon Nanospheres with Fast Sodium Storage Capability Enabled by Dominant Capacitive Behavior", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Microporous Carbon Nanospheres with Fast Sodium Storage Capability Enabled by Dominant Capacitive Behavior", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Microporous Carbon Nanospheres with Fast Sodium Storage Capability Enabled by Dominant Capacitive Behavior", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Microporous Carbon Nanospheres with Fast Sodium Storage Capability Enabled by Dominant Capacitive Behavior", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Microporous Carbon Nanospheres with Fast Sodium Storage Capability Enabled by Dominant Capacitive Behavior", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Microporous Carbon Nanospheres with Fast Sodium Storage Capability Enabled by Dominant Capacitive Behavior", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Microporous Carbon Nanospheres with Fast Sodium Storage Capability Enabled by Dominant Capacitive Behavior", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Microporous Carbon Nanospheres with Fast Sodium Storage Capability Enabled by Dominant Capacitive Behavior", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/medium/la2c00912_0008.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/large/la2c00912_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Hard carbon is considered one of the most promising anode candidates for sodium ion batteries but suffers from a moderate rate performance. Here, we design microporous carbon nanospheres using a novel hybrid monomer that simultaneously involves an organic moiety and an inorganic moiety as the starting unit. The inorganic moiety forms a continuous network, which serves as a 3D scaffold and a nanometer-scale template, then supports the off-collapse of the carbon skeleton and creates a well-developed microporous structure. In addition, the graphite microcrystal structure can be tailored by adjusting the heating treatment temperatures. The electrochemical study demonstrates that the microporous carbon nanospheres show dominant capacitive sodium storage behavior, thus presenting an outstanding rate performance. Even if a very high current density of 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> is applied, the hard carbon anode can deliver a large capacity of 127 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a considerable plateau capacity of 53 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which has rarely been obtained in previous publications. Besides, the carbon anode has a good cycling stability, and the capacity reached 210 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles with a current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, showing no dramatic capacity loss.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D7331%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DShuang%2BTang%252C%2BBaoshan%2BWan%252C%2BMinglu%2BZhang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D38%26issueNum%3D23%26contentID%3Dacs.langmuir.2c00912%26title%3DMicroporous%2BCarbon%2BNanospheres%2Bwith%2BFast%2BSodium%2BStorage%2BCapability%2BEnabled%2Bby%2BDominant%2BCapacitive%2BBehavior%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D0743-7463%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dlangd5%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D7340%26publicationDate%3DJune%2B2022" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2022 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrical%20properties" class="article__tags__link">Electrical properties</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292609&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanospheres" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nanospheres" class="article__tags__link">Nanospheres</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Sodium" class="article__tags__link">Sodium</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">With the rapid development of electric vehicles, smart grids, and portable electronic products, the demand for lithium-ion batteries is increasing; however, the scarcity and high price of lithium resources will bring crisis to the large-scale application of lithium-ion batteries in the future. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 1 to 9">(1−9)</a> Compared with lithium, sodium has the advantages of a wide distribution and abundant reserves, thus sodium-ion batteries attract significant attention and are regarded as potential substitutes for lithium-ion batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 10 to 15">(10−15)</a> Hard carbon has been considered one of the most promising anode candidates due to its advantages of a high sodium storage capacity and a low sodium storage potential. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 16 to 19">(16−19)</a> Basically, the charge–discharge curve of hard carbon used to store Na consists of two parts, one with a plateau region below 0.1 V and another with a slope region above 0.1 V. There are three main sodium storage modes in hard carbon materials: sodium adsorption at active sites on the surface, sodium storage by filling or adsorption within nanopores, and sodium storage by an intercalation reaction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 20 to 23">(20−23)</a> In this regard, current research is mainly devoted to improving the capacities of carbon electrode materials through expanding the distance between carbon layers, heteroatom doping, and optimizing the carbon microcrystal structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 24 to 28">(24−28)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Besides the specific capacity, the rate performance is important for sodium-ion batteries as it determines the output power. Normally, the electrochemical kinetics of metal-ion batteries can be divided as two types: surface-controlled capacitive behavior and diffusion-controlled battery-type behavior. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 29 and 30">(29,30)</a> The former always refers to the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> on the defects and the fast surface pseudocapacitive reaction, and the latter is a redox reaction in the bulk electrode. Capacitive storage has fast kinetics such that the response current is positively correlated to the scan rate in the cyclic voltammetry characterization. By contrary, the battery storage has sluggish kinetics such that the response current is positively correlated to the square root of the current. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> Interestingly, it was found that the battery-type energy storage behavior will be transformed into capacitive-type behavior when the electrode powder is downsized to nanometer scale. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> The increased accessible surface area of the electrode changes the electrochemical reaction in the bulk to that on the surface, leading to improved kinetics. On the other hand, the nanometer-scale size and well-developed porous structure are helpful to accelerate ion transfer in the electrode and supply buffer space during the sodiation and desodiation processes. Therefore, it is effective to exploit high-power electrode materials by downsizing the electrode to the nanometer-scale level and constructing nanopores in the electrode. On the basis of these considerations, a large number of porous carbon materials have been developed that present superior sodium storage performances. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 27, 33 and 34">(27,33,34)</a> However, it is still challenging to exploit a facile and effective method to construct a hard carbon anode with well-defined micropores and a good rate performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">The direct pyrolysis of polymer precursors is an effective and attractive strategy for designing carbon materials. Because of the diverse synthesis methods, the structure of the polymer is highly designable and controllable. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35,36)</a> In particular, the rapid development of “living/controllable” polymerization and surface-initiated polymerization in recent years has opened a wide avenue for synthesizing nanostructured carbons. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37,38)</a> However, many polymer precursors obtained by double-bond polymerization, such as polystyrene (PS), polypropylene (PP), polyethylene (PE), and poly(methyl methacrylate) (PMMA), are thermoplastic or have very low carbonization yields, preventing them from being converted to their corresponding carbon counterparts. Some publications reported the use of peroxidation, a concentrated H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> treatment, or some other techniques to strengthen the polymer backbone, enabling them to be carbonized to some linear polymers. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 39 and 40">(39,40)</a> However, these methods are still unable to overcome the structural disorganization induced by the thermoplastic nature intrinsic to these polymers and are thus incompetent in the design of nanocarbons.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this paper, microporous hard carbon nanospheres (MHCSs) with a uniform size and well-defined porous structure were prepared via emulsion polymerization using 3-(triethoxysilyl)propyl methacrylate (TPM) as the starting monomer. TPM has both organic and inorganic moieties simultaneously. The organic moiety provides the structural designability, and the inorganic moiety, which is converted to continuous SiO<sub><i>x</i></sub> during the preparation process, serves as rigid 3D scaffold to retain the nanostructure and a nanoscale template to create micropores. In addition, the graphite microcrystal structure of MHCS can be tailored by changing the heating treatment temperatures. With the combination of a regular nanomorphology, a well-defined pore structure, and a tailorable graphite microcrystal structure, the MHCS displays an attractive sodium storage performance, particularly a superior rate capability. Notably, for the MHCS electrode carbonized at 1300 °C, a high capacity of 227 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was achieved at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and the capacity remained 160 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under a current density of 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, giving a capacity retention of 70%. Even if a very high current density of 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> is applied, the hard carbon anode can deliver a large capacity of 127 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, suggesting a very short energy release time of &lt;1 min. In addition, the carbon anode presents a good cycling stability, as its capacity still reaches 210 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles with no visible capacity loss.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Materials Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">In a typical process, 10 mg of sodium dodecyl sulfate (SDS) was dissolved in 400 mL of H<sub>2</sub>O under mechanical stirring in a septum-sealed three-neck flask at room temperature. The solution was bubbled with N<sub>2</sub> for 30 min to remove air. After that, to the solution was added 10 mL of TPM using a syringe, and the mixture was bubbled for another 5 min. Subsequently, 0.1 g of potassium persulfate (K<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>O<sub>8</sub>) was quickly added to the mixture at 50 °C, starting the emulsion polymerization. After 10 h of reaction, the polymerization was terminated, and the mixture was cooled to room temperature. After centrifugation and being washed with water several times, the product was collected. The as-obtained white powder was dried at 60 °C for 12 h, then heated at 600 °C under a N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere for 2 h with a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> to pyrolyze the organic moiety to the carbon skeleton. The inorganic moiety was removed using HF acid, resulting in the formation of the microporous nanosphere (denoted as MHCS-600). After that, MHCS-600 was further calcined at different temperatures (800, 1000, and 1300 °C) for 2 h with a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> under a N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere, and a range of hard carbon materials was obtained (denoted as MHCS-<i>x</i>, where <i>x</i> represents the carbonization temperatures).</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The morphologies and microstructures of the materials were observed using scanning electron microscopy (SEM, SU8010, Hitachi, Japan) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEM-2010F, JEOL, Japan). The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were obtained by powder XRD (D/MAX-Ultima IV, Rigaku Corporation, Japan), and the Raman spectra were recorded using the LabRAM HR Evolution Raman spectrometer (Horiba Jobin Yvon, France). The specific surface area and the pore structure of the samples were obtained using the Micromeritics ASAP 2460 specific surface area and porosity analyzer (Micromeritics, USA) at 77 K. The surface area was calculated according to the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller equation, and the pore size distribution was determined using density functional theory (DFT).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical performance of the samples was analyzed by assembling CR 2032 coin-type batteries. MHCS-<i>x</i> as the active materials were used as working electrodes with an acetylene black conductor and a polyvinylidene fluoride binder in a weight ratio of 8:1:1. The prepared slurry was spread on the copper foil, then dried in a vacuum at 60 °C for 12 h. Sodium foil was used as the counter and reference electrodes, and a glass microfiber filters were used as separators. The electrolyte was a solution of 1 mol L<sup>–1</sup> NaPF6 in 1,2-dimethoxyethane (DME). The galvanostatic current charge–discharge tests were carried out in LAND-CT2001A battery testers (Wuhan LAND Corporation, China) within a voltage window of 0.01–3 V. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) tests were carried out using a CHI600E electrochemical workstation (Chenhua, Shanghai, China).</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The overall preparation process for the MHCSs is illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme 1</a>. In a typical process, the double bonds of the monomer TPM undergo an emulsion polymerization reaction to produce nanospheres at the beginning, followed by the hydrolysis–condensation reaction of siloxane to form a molecular-level bicontinuous polymer–SiO<sub><i>x</i></sub> composite (denoted as <i>p</i>-TPM). The detailed reaction process is displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/suppl_file/la2c00912_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>. SEM and TEM images of <i>p</i>-TPM are given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a and b, respectively, from which it can be seen that <i>p</i>-TPM presents a uniform spherical morphology with an average diameter of 116 nm. Meanwhile, SiO<sub><i>x</i></sub> is homogeneously distributed throughout the whole nanosphere and shows no visible aggregation. Then, <i>p</i>-TPM was carbonized at 600 °C under N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere, and the obtained sample was named <i>p</i>-TPM-600. TGA was used to investigate the carbonization behavior of <i>p</i>-TPM (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/suppl_file/la2c00912_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). According to the thermal decomposition curve under a N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere, the carbonization yield of the organic component was calculated to be 12%. As a result, the carbon and SiO<sub><i>x</i></sub> contents in <i>p</i>-TPM-600 are 28.4% and 71.6%, respectively. <i>p</i>-TPM-600 has spherical nanomorphology similar to that of its macromolecular precursor <i>p</i>-TPM, but the nanospheres are slightly adhered to each other due to partial sintering during the high-temperature heating treatment, (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/suppl_file/la2c00912_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). It is well-known that polymers obtained by double-bond polymerization are usually thermoplastic and have very low carbon residue rates (e.g., PMMA, PS, PE, and PP), which makes it difficult to use them to fabricate carbon materials. In the present study, it is believed that the SiO<sub><i>x</i></sub> part, which forms a rigid 3D inorganic framework in the carbon precursor, is critical to keep the spherical morphology from being pulverized. To confirm this, we prepared a control sample by etching the SiO<sub><i>x</i></sub> before carbonization, and finally only irregular carbon powders were obtained (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/suppl_file/la2c00912_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>).</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/medium/la2c00912_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/large/la2c00912_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Schematic Diagram for the Fabrication of MHCS-<i>x</i> Nanospheres</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/large/la2c00912_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/medium/la2c00912_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/large/la2c00912_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) SEM and (b) TEM images of <i>p</i>-TPM. (c) SEM and (d) low-magnification TEM images of MHCS-600. (e–h) High-magnification TEM images of MHCS under the following different carbonization temperatures: (e) MHCS-600, (f) MHCS-800, (g) MHCS-1000, and (h) MHCS-1300.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/large/la2c00912_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The target carbon samples are obtained by acid extracting the SiO<sub><i>x</i></sub> template from <i>p</i>-TPM-600 and further heat treating the samples at the predicted temperatures. The carbons are denoted as MHCS-<i>x</i>, where <i>x</i> represents the carbonization temperature. The SEM and low-magnification TEM images of MHCS-600 are given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c and d, respectively. As shown, the nanomorphology exhibits no obvious change after the elimination of the template, and these MHCS-600 nanospheres have an average diameter of approximately 125 nm. The sphere diameters were nearly the same when the carbonization temperature was further increased to 800, 1000, and 1300 °C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/suppl_file/la2c00912_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). Interestingly, the carbon layer structure can be well-tailored by adjusting the temperature. As displayed in the high-magnification TEM images in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e–h, MHCS-600 has very short carbon layers with randomly oriented carbon lattice fringes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> As the carbonization temperature increases, these carbon layers become longer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> In particular, when the carbonization temperature reached 1300 °C, the carbon layers were significantly lengthened, and the turbostratic carbon structure could be observed. In the meantime, the long carbon layers naturally roll up, leading to the formation of some closed-pore domains. These closed pores are believed to be important for sodium storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The carbon microcrystal structures of all materials were further measured using the XRD and Raman spectra. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a, all the MHCS samples show broad peaks at ≈23 and 43°, corresponding to the diffraction bands of the (002) and (101) planes of graphite, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> Such broad peaks present the typical characteristic of amorphous carbons, demonstrating that the graphitization degrees of the MHCS samples are relatively low. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> In addition, the position of the (002) peak shifts slightly toward a higher angle as the pyrolysis temperature increases, indicating a reduction in the distance between the carbon layers. According to the Bragg law, the calculated interlayer distances are 0.382, 0.377, 0.374, and 0.370 nm for MHCS-600, MHCS-800, MHCS-1000, and MHCS-1300, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b and <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>). Meanwhile, the microcrystal size along <i>c</i>-axis (<i>L</i><sub>c</sub>) increases from 1.262 nm for MHCS-600 to 1.311 nm for MHCS-1300.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/medium/la2c00912_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/large/la2c00912_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Structural characterization results of obtained MHCS samples. (a) XRD patterns, (b) d<sub>002</sub> spacings, (c) Raman spectra, (d) <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> values, (e) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms, and (f) DFT pore size distributions.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/large/la2c00912_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Structural Parameters of Prepared Carbon Samples and Corresponding Precursors</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">sample</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">d<sub>002</sub></th><th class="colsep0
