class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.262</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.74</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">301</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">79</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.296</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">MHCS-800</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.377</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.291</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.44</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">288</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">209</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.308</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">MHCS-1000</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.374</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.300</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.07</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">680</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">506</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.462</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">MHCS-1300</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.370</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.311</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.65</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">101</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">30</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.14</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">The structural evolution is also confirmed by the Raman spectra in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c. The characteristic bands at ≈1345 cm<sup>–1</sup> (D-band) and 1590 cm<sup>–1</sup> (G-band) correspond to the disordered and graphitic structures, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref45" aria-label="reference 21 and 45">(21,45)</a> The intensity ratio of the D-band to the G-band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) is indicative of structural defects in the carbon framework and is usually used to evaluate the degree of order for carbon materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 46 and 47">(46,47)</a> To obtain <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> values, Raman spectra were deconvoluted into four Lorentz peaks, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/suppl_file/la2c00912_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>; the corresponding <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> values are depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d. As the pyrolysis temperature increases, the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> values for MHCS-600, MHCS-800, MHCS-1000, and MHCS-1300 tend to gradually decrease, which indicates a decrease in structural defects, and the ordering degree of the carbon layers gradually increases. These results are in good agreement with TEM observations and the XRD analysis.</div><div class="NLM_p">The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption characterization was used to evaluate the pore structures of the MHCS samples. As can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e, the adsorption amount of <i>p</i>-TPM-600 increases at low relative pressure (<i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub>), demonstrating the existence of micropores. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> After that, a hysteresis loop appears, indicating the formation of mesopores. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> Obviously, the micropores are the intrinsic pores in the carbon framework generated during the high-temperature carbonization process, and the mesopores originate from the stacking voids of carbon spheres. After the etching of the SiO<sub><i>x</i></sub> template, the adsorption amount of the carbon counterpart (MHCS-600) at low relative pressure dramatically increased, suggesting a large number of new micropores were created. Accordingly, the BET surface area increased from 132 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> for <i>p</i>-TPM-600 to 301 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> for MHCS-600. For elevated temperatures of 800 and 1000 °C, the surface area further increases to 288 and 680 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> respectively, owing to the continuously decomposition of the carbon skeleton. As the temperature reaches 1300 °C, the turbostratic arrangement of carbon layers reduces the number of nanopores, and the specific surface area subsequently falls sharply to 101 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>.</div><div class="NLM_p">Owing to its unique structural merits, MHCS is considered to have a large electrode–electrolyte contact area, an accelerated ion diffusion speed, and a shortened ion transfer length, thus presenting a superior sodium storage performance. To prove this, the sodium storage properties of MHCS samples were studied in CR2032 coin-type batteries, with sodium metal as the reference electrode and 1 mol L<sup>–1</sup> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME as the electrolyte. The first three-cycle galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) curves of the obtained carbon materials at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a–d. The GCD curves of MHCS-600, MHCS-800, and MHCS-1000 show only slope regions without any obvious plateaus. On contrast, MHCS-1300 presents typical GCD curves, incorporating a slope region under high potentials (&gt;0.1 V) and a plateau region under low potentials (&lt;0.1 V). This means that MHCS-600, MHCS-800, and MHCS-1000 store Na<sup>+</sup> mainly in the capacitive way, while MHCS-1300 stores Na<sup>+</sup> in both the capacitive and battery ways at the same time. Owing to the long plateau capacity, MHCS-1300 provides the highest reversible capacity of 230 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, surpassing MHCS-600 (132 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), MHCS-800 (147 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), and MHCS-1000 (152 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). In agreement with the GCD results, the CV curves of MHCS-600, MHCS-800, and MHCS-1000 have no strong redox peaks, but those of MHCS-1300 display a pair of sharp peaks around 0.1 V that correspond to the voltage plateau in the GCD curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e–h).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/medium/la2c00912_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/large/la2c00912_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a–d) The charge–discharge curves for the initial three cycles of (a) MHCS-600, (b) MHCS-800, (c) MHCS-1000, and (d) MHCS-1300 at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (e–h) The initial three cyclic voltammograms of (e) MHCS-600, (f) MHCS-800, (g) MHCS-1000, and (h) MHCS-1300 over a voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V at the scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/large/la2c00912_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Normally, the plateau capacity is attributed to the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> in carbon layers or Na filling in the closed pores. Until now, the exact mechanism remained in debate. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 49 to 52">(49−52)</a> Here, to investigate the origin of the plateau for MHCS-1300, we studied the electrode state after the sodiation process. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a presents the dark-field TEM image and corresponding elemental mapping images of MHCS-1300 after the initial discharge to 0.01 V. To eliminate electrolyte interference, the carbon electrode was cleaned several times with diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (≥99.5%) prior to the TEM characterization. The results show that after the initial discharge the MHCS-1300 electrode consists of C, O, Na, and P. Clearly, the P element is from the residual electrolyte NaPF<sub>6</sub>. The P content is much lower than the Na content, suggesting that most of the Na is stored in the carbon framework. The high-magnification TEM images of the unsodiated and sodiated electrode are given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b. The unsodiated carbon has a typical turbostratic structure, with an average interlayer spacing of approximately 0.376 nm. After sodiation, the microcrystal structure of the carbon electrode shows no visible change, and the interlayer spacing is maintained at approximately 0.378 nm, very close to that of the unsodiated carbon electrode. This result indicates that the intercalation reaction of Na<sup>+</sup> in the carbon layer is not dominated in the plateau region. In other words, we believe that the filling of the pseudometallic Na or Na cluster in the closed pores mainly contributes to the plateau capacity.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/medium/la2c00912_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/large/la2c00912_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Dark-filed TEM image and corresponding elemental mappings of MHCS-1300 after an initial discharge to 0.01 V. (b) High-magnification TEM images of MHCS-1300 before and after the initial discharge (the right insets are the interplanar spacings).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/large/la2c00912_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">MHCS-<i>x</i> presents a superior rate performance. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a, MHCS-1300 delivers reversible capacities of 227, 217, 204, 194, 182, and 160 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, showing an outstanding capacity retention regardless of low or high current density. Remarkably, it maintains a large capacity of 127 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> even at an ultrahigh current density of 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, giving a capacity retention of 56% against the capacity at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Such a high rate performance means that MHCS-1300 is able to release its total energy in a very short time of &lt;1 min, which is suitable for high-power-output devices. When the current density shifts back to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity of MHCS-1300 returns to 225 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with no visible capacity loss. To confirm the good rate performance, we recorded the GCD curves of MHCS-1300 under different current densities, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b. As we know, it is normal for the plateau capacity to decline rapidly as the current density increases, and under an ultrafast charge–discharge rate, e.g., 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the plateau region almost disappears. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53 ref54 ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53 ref54 ref55" aria-label="reference 53 to 55">(53−55)</a> However, here MHCS-1300 has relatively long plateau region no matter how high the current density is. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c, the plateau capacity reaches 53 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under a current density of 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, giving a capacity retention of 52% against the plateau capacity under a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The good rate capability of the plateau capacity is the key to endowing MHCS-1300 with the fast sodium storage property. To further confirm the superiority of the rate capability, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d compares the specific capacities under different current densities of MHCS-1300 with some recent hard carbon anodes, including lignocellulose biomass-derived hard carbon (SP-1300), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> nanofibrous hard carbon fabric (s-HCNF), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> sweet-gum-derived carbon (SGHC-1000), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> N,O-codoped hard carbon (NOHPHC), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> and highly disordered carbon (HDC). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> As can be seen, the capacity of MHCS-1300 under high current densities is larger than or comparable those of most of these hard carbon anodes.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/medium/la2c00912_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/large/la2c00912_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Rate performance of MHCS samples. (b) GCD curves of MHCS-1300 under different current densities. (c) Total capacities, sloping capacities, and plateau capacities of MHCS-1300 under different current densities. (d) Comparison of the rate capability of MHCS-1300 with those of other recently reported carbon-based electrodes. (e) Cycling performance of MHCS-<i>x</i> at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (f) Nyquist plots of the MHCS-1300 electrode after 1, 5, 10, 20, 50, and 100 cycles in comparison with the fresh electrode. (g) Long-term cycling performance of MHCS-<i>x</i> with a a current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/large/la2c00912_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Besides their superior rate performance, MHCS samples also have good cycling stabilities. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e depicts the reversible capacities of the carbon electrodes under a current density 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The results reveal that the capacities experienced no dramatic degradation over the whole test process. After 100 repeated charge–discharge cycles, MHCS-1300 still delivered a high capacity of 245 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, nearly equal to the capacity of the second cycle (249 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). Similar results were obtained for other MHCS samples. We also characterized the microscopy morphology and microcrystal structure of the cycled MHCS-1300 electrode by SEM and XRD to confirm its structural stability. As can be seen in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/suppl_file/la2c00912_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S7 and S8</a>, the spherical morphology was retained and the interlayer spacing exhibited no dramatic change after 100 cycles. Furthermore, EIS was used to characterize the electrode durability during the cycling process. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f, the diameter of the semicircle, i.e., the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), experienced no sudden increase but gradually became smaller with the cycling measurement. This result indicates that the carbon electrode is stable and an electrochemical activation occurs, making the electrode is more accessible for the Na<sup>+</sup>.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further confirm the long-term durability, the MHCS electrodes were repeatedly charged and discharged 1000 times at a current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g). All samples showed outstanding long-term cycling stabilities, with no dramatic capacity loss during the whole testing process. For example, the specific capacity of MHCS-1300 slightly declined in the initial 50 cycles and then became very stable. From the 50th cycle to the final 1000th cycle, the capacity dropped from 235 to 210 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, giving a capacity retention of 89% and a very low capacity loss rate of 0.011% per cycle. Additionaly, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/suppl_file/la2c00912_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>, MHCS-1300 still shows a high reversible capacity of 193 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> even after 2000 cycles. As for the other MHCS samples MHCS-600, MHCS-800, and MHCS-1000, owing to their relatively lower specific capacities along and slighter volume changes, their cycling stabilities were ever better and the capacities were nearly unchanged in the long-term repeated charge–discharge tests.</div><div class="NLM_p">To clarify the high rate performance of MHCS-1300, CV measurements at different scan rates were carried out to investigate the electrochemical reaction kinetics of MHCS-1300 in detail. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a displays the CV curves of MHCS-1300 measured at different scan rates. It is obvious that the potential difference between the redox peaks is insignificant, indicating that the sodium ion produces little polarization during the insertion and extraction processes. To determine whether the charge storage kinetics was dominated by a fast capacitance-controlled reaction or a slow diffusion-controlled battery-type reaction, the following equation was used: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58 ref59 ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58 ref59 ref60" aria-label="reference 58 to 60">(58−60)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/medium/la2c00912_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i10"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span></div><figure id="fig6" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/medium/la2c00912_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/large/la2c00912_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) CV curves of MHCS-1300 under different scan rates. (b) <i>b</i>-Values of cathodic and anodic peaks in the log(peak current) vs log(scan rate) plot. (c) Capacitive contribution to the total capacity of MHCS-1300 with a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Capacitive contributions of MHCS-1300 at different scan rates. (e) GITT curves of MHCS samples. (f) Diffusion coefficients of MHCS samples calculated using the GITT results.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/large/la2c00912_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">This equation can be transformed into the following form:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/medium/la2c00912_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i11"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span>where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are constants, <i>i</i> is the real-time current, and <i>v</i> is the scan rate. Normally, the slope <i>b</i> is close to 1, demonstrating a fast capacitance-controlled process; in contrast , a small <i>b</i>-value that approaches to 0.5 is indicative of a slow diffusion-controlled battery-type process. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b shows the fits of the peak current variations of the cathodic and anodic peaks at different scan rates. The obtained slope values (<i>b</i>) are 0.78 and 0.93 for the anodic and cathodic peaks (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b), respectively, suggesting that the capacitive energy storage process is dominant. Notably, these <i>b</i>-values are higher than most of current published results, which suggests that MHCS has faster electrochemical kinetics enabled by its unique spherical morphology and well-developed microporous structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref53 ref61 ref62 ref63 ref64'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref53 ref61 ref62 ref63 ref64" aria-label="reference 10, 53 and 61 to 64">(10,53,61−64)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The capacitive contribution was further qualitatively calculated using the equation <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref65 ref66'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref65 ref66" aria-label="reference 32, 65 and 66">(32,65,66)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/asset/images/medium/la2c00912_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i12"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span>where the <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> component represents the capacitive contribution and the <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i> component represents the battery contribution. The calculated capacitive contributions are 58%, 71%, 79%, 83%, and 85% for different scan rates of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 0.8, and 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 6</a>c and d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/suppl_file/la2c00912_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S10</a>). Such a high capacitive contribution to the total capacity endows MHCS electrode with fast electrochemical kinetics and an accompanying impressive high-rate capability.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Galvanostatic intermittent titration (GITT) was also employed to study the sodium storage kinetics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53 ref67 ref68'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53 ref67 ref68" aria-label="reference 53, 67 and 68">(53,67,68)</a> As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>e, for the GITT test, the carbon electrodes were first discharged with a pulse current of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for 10 min, followed by 2 h of relaxation. The diffusion coefficients of Na<sup>+</sup> (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) in the carbon electrodes were calculated using Fick’s second law, and the results are depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>f. The D<sub>Na+</sub> values of all the carbon electrodes are on the order of 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, which is higher than or comparable to those of most current carbon anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref69 ref70 ref71'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref69 ref70 ref71" aria-label="reference 69 to 71">(69−71)</a> These high diffusion coefficients demonstrate the fast Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics in the carbon electrodes. Moreover, in consideration of the similar Na<sup>+</sup> storage reactions and nanomorphology, the D<sub>Na+</sub> value in the present study was determined by the conductivity and the porous structure. The conductivity is indicative of the degree of graphitization, and the porous structure is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e and f and <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>. Therefore, MHCS-600 with the lowest graphitization degree and MHCS-1300 with the lowest surface area present relatively small diffusion coefficients.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In conclusion, a novel hybrid monomer that simultaneously involves an organic moiety and an inorganic moiety was used as starting material to construct microporous carbon spheres. The inorganic-moiety-derived SiO<sub><i>x</i></sub> network simultaneously serves as rigid 3D scaffold and a nanoscale template, which is the key to the synthesis of the carbon spheres. The experimental results show that the microporous carbon spheres have uniform sizes at approximately 125 nm and display a high surface area with well-defined micropores. Meanwhile, the graphite microcrystal texture can be tailored by adjusting the carbonization temperatures. Owing to these unique structural merits, microporous carbon spheres present dominant capacitive sodium storage behavior, with an impressive high-rate capability. MHCS-1300 offers a high reversible capacity of 261 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and retains 210 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Even if a very high current density of 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> is applied, the hard carbon anode can deliver a large capacity of 127 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. The developed method here overcomes the thermal instability of carbon precursors obtained by double-bond polymerization, which may open a new avenue for the design of nanostructured carbons. Meanwhile, the current study offers a useful design for a high-rate carbon anode for sodium storage and is believed to facilitate new opportunities for the practical application of sodium ion batteries.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">The reaction process for the preparation of <i>p</i>-TPM, scanning electron micrographs, XRD characterization plots, and pseudocapacitance calculation plots (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/suppl_file/la2c00912_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912/suppl_file/la2c00912_si_001.pdf">la2c00912_si_001.pdf (1.21 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acs.langmuir.2c00912" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrZwDymuubZm7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj4H_XByS3kLQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhsVGktL%252FJ&amp;md5=5035bfda7631ad9d075e8836e61146ef</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research development on sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                 10.1021/cr500192f</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D114%26publication_year%3D2014%26pages%3D11636-11682%26journal%3DChem.%2BRev.%26issue%3D23%26author%3DN.%2BYabuuchi%26author%3DK.%2BKubota%26author%3DM.%2BDahbi%26author%3DS.%2BKomaba%26title%3DResearch%2Bdevelopment%2Bon%2Bsodium-ion%2Bbatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fcr500192f&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.langmuir.2c00912&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fcr500192f&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of sodium-ion battery technol.  Future outlook of sodium-ion batteries is discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo_Iht8id70u7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liCkJSbiPGSzg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvFSiu7bL&amp;md5=d4497339c61083b2ec2c0568261e4909</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.9b01768"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gomez-Martin, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Martinez-Fernandez, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ruttert, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Winter, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Placke, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ramirez-Rico, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Correlation of Structure and Performance of Hard Carbons as Anodes for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>31</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">18</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">7288</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">7299</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Correlation of Structure and Performance of Hard Carbons as Anodes for Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gomez-Martin, Aurora; Martinez-Fernandez, Julian; Ruttert, Mirco; Winter, Martin; Placke, Tobias; Ramirez-Rico, Joaquin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">31</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">18</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7288-7299</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbons are the material of choice as neg. electrode in sodium ion batteries.  Despite being extensively studied, there is still debate regarding the mechanisms responsible for storage in low- and high-potential regions.  This work presents a comprehensive approach to elucidate the involved storage mechanisms when Na ions insert into such disordered structures.  Synchrotron X-ray total scattering expts. were performed to access quant. information on at. ordering in these materials at the nanoscale.  Results prove that hard carbons undergo an at. rearrangement as the graphene layers cross-link at intermediate temps. (1200-1600 °C), resulting in an increase of the av. interplanar distance up to 1400 °C, followed by a progressive decrease.  This increase correlates with the pos. trend in the reversible capacity of biomass-derived carbons when processed up to 1200-1600 °C due to an increased capacity at low potential (≤0.1 V vs Na/Na+).  A decrease in achievable sloping capacity with increasing heat-treatment temp. arises from larger cryst. domains and a lower concn. of defects.  The obsd. correlation between structural parameters and electrochem. properties clearly supports that the main storage of Na ions into a hard-carbon structure is based on an adsorption-intercalation mechanism.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpczqneul94i7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liCkJSbiPGSzg"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Unraveling the Voltage Failure Mechanism in Metal Sulfide Anodes for Sodium Storage and Improving Their Long Cycle Life by Sulfur-Doped Carbon Protection</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Fei; Han, Fei; He, Yulong; Zhang, Jian; Wu, Huang; Tao, Ji; Zhang, Chengzhi; Zhang, Fuquan; Liu, Jinshui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">31</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2007266</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Metal sulfides are emerging as a promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries with high reversible capacities and fast reaction kinetics, but achieving long-cycling-life remains a great challenge.  Here, taking cobalt sulfide as an example, its electrochem. sodium-ion storage failure phenomenon is first reported, which indicates that the battery cannot reach the cut-off voltage during charging.  Detailed analyses demonstrate that such failure may originate from the dissoln. and escape of polysulfide intermediates, further reacting with the released copper-ions from the current collector and inducing the occurrence of the shuttle effect.  Based on the explored failure mechanism, a sulfur-doped carbon matrix with polar carbon sulfur bonds, which can firmly immobilize the dissolved polysulfides, is deliberately introduced into the Co1-xS active particles (Co1-xS/s-C) to improve their cycle stability.  Consequently, the cycle life of the Co1-xS/s-C anode for sodium-ion storage is extended from the original 125 to present 2000 cycles, even at high-rate current densities.  Moreover, utilizing the carbon current collector instead of traditional copper can effectively delay the occurrence of the failure phenomenon.  The present work promotes better fundamental understanding of the structural evolution of metal sulfide anodes during cycles, and the soln. strategy can be extended to apply in other metal sulfides (ZnS, NiS).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrP-LsQulAfoLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liCkJSbiPGSzg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXitVWrurjJ&amp;md5=cdab5c6a282f7b3e32cc131b312a1222</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2017.03.026"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Si, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bao, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Chemical bonding between antimony and ionic liquid-derived nitrogen-doped carbon for sodium-ion battery anode</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Chemical bonding between antimony and ionic liquid-derived nitrogen-doped carbon for sodium-ion battery anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Xin; Si, Ling; Zhou, Xiaosi; Tu, Fengzhang; Zhu, Xiaoshu; Bao, Jianchun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">349</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Antimony has received a great deal of attention as a promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to its high theor. capacity of 660 mAh g-1.  However, this application is significantly hampered by inherent large vol. change and sluggish kinetics.  To address these issues, an antimony-cyano-based ionic liq.-derived nitrogen-doped carbon (Sb-CNC) hybrid is proposed and synthesized by ball-milling and subsequent pyrolysis treatment.  As an anode material for SIBs, the as-synthesized Sb-CNC hybrid delivers reversible capacities of 475 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1 and 203 mAh g-1 at 5000 mA g-1, and a 92.4% capacity retention based on the first-cycle capacity after 150 cycles at 100 mA g-1.  Using ex situ XPS and elemental mapping techniques, we attribute the good structural integrity to the formation of Sb-N-C bonds between Sb and the cyano-based ionic liq.-derived N-doped carbon matrix.  Moreover, the presence of N-doped carbon network in the hybrid material serves as a robust protective cover and an elec. highway, buffering the substantial vol. expansion of Sb nanoparticles and ensuring the fast electron transport for stable cycling operation.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq0rTJNqWEUn7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liCkJSbiPGSzg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXktVKmsLc%253D&amp;md5=bdba7a23cb25ed9d8053b89fd94d27e2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1016/j.jallcom.2018.07.082"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bao, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">An efficient sodium-ion battery consisting of reduced graphene oxide bonded Na3V2(PO4)3 in a composite carbon network</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">An efficient sodium-ion battery consisting of reduced graphene oxide bonded Na3V2(PO4)3 in a composite carbon network</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gu, Erlong; Liu, Shuhu; Zhang, Zhuangzhuang; Fang, Yuyan; Zhou, Xiaosi; Bao, Jianchun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Alloys and Compounds</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">767</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have been considered as the most promising candidate for large-scale energy storage applications.  However, the poor cycling stability and inferior rate capability of existing cathode materials for Na-ion batteries restrict future developments.  Herein, the authors report a chem. bonded cathode material for Na-ion batteries that is synthesized by freeze-drying and subsequent annealing to generate Na3V2(PO4)3/reduced graphene oxide-C nanotubes (NGC) composite.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The overall electrochem. performance of carbon anodes for potassium-ion batteries (PIBs) is considerably restricted by their poor cyclic property and rate capability issues.  Herein, we report a water chestnut-derived slope-dominated carbon anode through moderate-temp. pyrolysis at 900°C, which manifests a high initial reversible capacity of 253.2 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1, remarkable rate property of 134.8 mAh g-1 at 1000 mA g-1, and impressive cycling performance of 220.5 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1 after 1000 cycles.  When assembled with the potassiated 3,4,9,10-perylene-tetracarboxylic acid dianhydride cathode, the initial reversible capacity reaches 124.3 mAh g-1 at 25 mA g-1, and 84.7% of the capacity is maintained even after 300 cycles at 50 mA g-1.  The improved potassium storage properties could be ascribed to the disordered microstructure with relatively larger oxygen-contg. defect concn., high sp. surface area, and stochastically oriented short graphene nanosheets.  D. functional theory calcns. illustrate that oxygen doping can effectively alter the charge d. distribution of carbon and facilitate the adsorption of K+ on water chestnut-derived carbon, which boosts potassium ion storage.  This work would provide a promising avenue to design and synthesize slope-dominated carbon materials for low-cost PIBs with excellent rate and cycling properties and high safety.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoHXq3NdmHd5bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh8gP3x_W0dKQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXit1Kru7fJ&amp;md5=91ad90edcb22a90feba25a17aeae1b8e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201902657"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, S.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">SnO2 as Advanced Anode of Alkali-Ion Batteries: Inhibiting Sn Coarsening by Crafting Robust Physical Barriers, Void Boundaries, and Heterophase Interfaces for Superior Electrochemical Reaction Reversibility</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Shiqiang; Sewell, Christopher D.; Liu, Ruiping; Jia, Songru; Wang, Zewei; He, Yanjie; Yuan, Kunjie; Jin, Huile; Wang, Shun; Liu, Xueqin; Lin, Zhiqun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1902657</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Superior reaction reversibility of electrode materials is urgently pursued for improving the energy d. and lifespan of batteries.  Tin dioxide (SnO2) is a promising anode material for alkali-ion batteries, having a high theor. lithium storage capacity of 1494 mAh g- based on the reactions of SnO2 + 4Li+ + 4e- ↔ Sn + 2Li2O and Sn + 4.4Li+ + 4.4e- ↔ Li4.4Sn.  The coarsening of Sn nanoparticles into large particles induced reaction reversibility degrdn. has been demonstrated as the essential failure mechanism of SnO2 electrodes.  Here, three key strategies for inhibiting Sn coarsening to enhance the reaction reversibility of SnO2 are presented.  First, encapsulating SnO2 nanoparticles in phys. barriers of carbonaceous materials, conductive polymers or inorg. materials can robustly prevent Sn coarsening among the wrapped SnO2 nanoparticles.  Third, fabricating SnO2-based heterogeneous composites consisting of metals, metal oxides or metal sulfides can introduce abundant heterophase interfaces in cycled electrodes that impede Sn coarsening among the isolated SnO2 cryst. domains.  Finally, a perspective on the future prospect of the structural/compositional designs of SnO2 as anode of alkali-ion batteries is highlighted.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrtrEwiy-_yybVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh8gP3x_W0dKQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXitl2nsb7N&amp;md5=2fe3aae2e09a2350e1cb7bdfd90453c7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201901093"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Harn, Y.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Superresilient Hard Carbon Nanofabrics for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ding, Chenfeng; Huang, Lingbo; Lan, Jinle; Yu, Yunhua; Zhong, Wei-Hong; Yang, Xiaoping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">16</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1906883</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Developing supermechanically resilient hard carbon materials that can quickly accommodate sodium ions is highly demanded in fabricating durable anodes for wearable sodium-ion batteries.  Here, an interconnected spiral nanofibrous hard carbon fabric with both remarkable resiliency (e.g., recovery rate as high as 1200 mm s-1) and high Young's modulus is reported.  The hard carbon nanofabrics are prepd. by spinning and then carbonizing the reaction product of polyacrylonitrile and polar mols. (melamine).  The resulting unique hard carbon possesses a highly disordered carbonaceous structure with enlarged interlayer spacing contributed from the strong electrostatic repulsion of dense pyrrolic nitrogen atoms.  Its excellent resiliency remains after intercalation/deintercalation of sodium ions.  The outstanding sodium-storage performance of the derived anode includes excellent gravimetric capacity, high-power capability, and long-term cyclic stability.  More significantly, with a high loading mass, the hard carbon anode displays a high-power capacity (1.05 mAh cm-2 at 2 A g-1) and excellent cyclic stability.  This study provides a unique strategy for the design and fabrication of new hard carbon materials for advanced wearable energy storage systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoWCR5Ae43zHLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh8gP3x_W0dKQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXjs1Kqs70%253D&amp;md5=f3fb721aad7739d13eabb9ba14cbda44</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202000283"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Alvin, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cahyadi, H.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Revealing the Intercalation Mechanisms of Lithium, Sodium, and Potassium in Hard Carbon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Alvin, Stevanus; Cahyadi, Handi Setiadi; Hwang, Jieun; Chang, Wonyoung; Kwak, Sang Kyu; Kim, Jaehoon</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">20</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2000283</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon is the most promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries and potassium-ion batteries owing to its high stability, widespread availability, low-cost, and excellent performance.  Understanding the carrier-ion storage mechanism is a prerequisite for developing high-performance electrode materials; however, the underlying ion storage mechanism in hard carbon has been a topic of debate because of its complex structure.  Herein, it is demonstrated that the Li+-, Na+-, and K+-ion storage mechanisms in hard carbon are based on the adsorption of ions on the surface of active sites (e.g., deffects, edges, and residual heteroatoms) in the sloping voltage region, followed by intercalation into the graphitic layers in the low-voltage plateau region.  At a low c.d. of 3 mA g-1, the graphitic layers of hard carbon are unlocked to permit Li+-ion intercalation, resulting in a plateau region in the lithium-ion batteries.  To gain insights into the ion storage mechanism, exptl. observations including various ex situ techniques, a const.-current const.-voltage method, and diffusivity measurements are correlated with the theor. estn. of changes in carbon structures and insertion voltages during ion insertion obtained using the d. functional theory.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo3nciM-7eFM7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh8gP3x_W0dKQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXntVOqsr4%253D&amp;md5=4a410f22779904b797847b465d1c4a5b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2019.05.008"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, H.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Three-dimensional porous carbon doped with N, O and P heteroatoms as high-performance anode materials for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhou, Chunli; Wang, Dengke; Li, Ang; Pan, Erzhuang; Liu, Haiyan; Chen, Xiaohong; Jia, Mengqiu; Song, Huaihe</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Porous C (PC) materials are the excellent alternative to anodes of high performance Na ion batteries (SIBs).  However, most PC materials were prepd. with complicated and multi-step template method, which limits the mass prodn.  Herein, the authors present a simple and repeatable calcination approach to synthesize three-dimensional porous C (3-dimensional-PC) materials using poly(p-phenylenediamine) hydrogel as precursor.  N, O and P heteroatoms are successfully introduced into 3-dimensional-PC to provide more Na storage sites.  The obtained 3-dimensional-PC delivers high Na storage capacity of 332 mAh g-1 at 50 mA g-1 and the reversible capacity still retains 139 mAh g-1 at 10 A g-1.  Also, 3-dimensional-PC also shows ultra-long cycling life, the stable capacities of 212 mAh g-1 and 120 mAh g-1 after 1000 cycles even at the high c.d. of 0.5 A g-1 and 5 A g-1, resp.  These results illustrate that 3-dimensional-PC electrode displays great rate capability and excellent cycling stability, which would be ascribed to surface-dominated Na storage mechanism according to quant. anal.  This method is low cost and effective for prepg. high-performance porous C anodes for SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoiwmbDkp4jtrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixAtt8EymWUQ"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Passionfruit-Like Carbon-Confined Cu2ZnSnS4 Anode for Ultralong-Life Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Bing; Zhang, Qin; Zhang, Chengzhi; Xu, Wenli; Wang, Jiapei; Yuan, Guanming; Lv, Wei; Li, Xuanke; Yang, Nianjun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">17</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2100082</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The sodium-ion battery (SIB) is one of the new generation of electrochem. energy storage devices, although its life-time needs to be improved.  To assemble an SIB with an ultralong life-time, passionfruit-like Cu2ZnSnS4 nanoparticles confined with nitrogen-doped carbon (CZTS@C) are synthesized.  As an alternative anode for an SIB, the CZTS@C anode exhibits a reversible capacity of 461 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 50 mA g-1 after 140 charge/discharge cycles and excellent cycling stability (e.g., a capacity of 146 mA h g-1 at 4 A g-1 even after 1000 charge/discharge cycles).  Such high performance originates from multiple electrons involved in the electrochem. reactions of the CZTS@C anode, as verified by in situ X-ray diffraction measurements.  As a conductive maskant, the carbon shell enhances the elec. cond. of the CZTS@C anode and further behaves as a blocked layer to maintain the structural integrity of this anode, namely to avoid the loss of active components.  Several heterostructures that promote the reaction kinetics of the Na+ storage are formed in the subsequent charging process, as proved by ex situ transmission electron microscopy.  The remarkable performance based on this low-cost CZTS anode may pave the way to develop com. SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr_WZ6RwJRDSrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixAtt8EymWUQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXmt1eqsrg%253D&amp;md5=fd3648e99e9e46f0a43717ba04707217</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1039/C8TA11510A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dravid, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium storage in hard carbon with curved graphene platelets as the basic structural units</span>. <i>Journal of Materials Chemistry A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">7</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">3327</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3335</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium storage in hard carbon with curved graphene platelets as the basic structural units</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Ke; Xu, Yaobin; Li, Yuan; Dravid, Vinayak; Wu, Jinsong; Huang, Ying</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3327-3335</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon is demonstrated as an anode material of high capacity and cycling stability in sodium ion batteries (SIBs).  However, the structure uncertainty of hard carbon and lack of understanding about its sodium-storage mechanism have limited its further optimization.  Here, we have studied the at. structures of pristine and sodiated hard carbon by in situ and ex situ transmission electron microscopy and correlated the microstructures with their electrochem. properties.  Unlike graphite with its almost flat graphene layers, the graphene layers in hard carbon are normally curved.  Such curved graphene layers form a platelet as the basic structural unit and the platelets pack randomly to form a 3D porous structure.  As the graphene layers are curved and misaligned, their interlayer distance is large and suitable for Na-ions intercalation.  During in situ sodiation, an obvious vol. expansion that resulted from Na intercalation is obsd. near the end of sodiation, implying that intercalation of Na into the curved graphene platelets (corresponding to the voltage plateau ∼0.15 V) occurs after Na absorption in the surface/defect absorption (corresponding to a voltage slope from ∼2 to 0.15 V).  Our investigation will provide insights for designing and fabricating more effective hard carbon for next generation SIBs with high capacity and cyclability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrReW8sWFhzE7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixAtt8EymWUQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXptVSjsQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=98712f50d48bb319bcc99b9f58994879</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1149/1.1393348"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stevens, D.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High capacity anode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Stevens, D. A.; Dahn, J. R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. techniques have been used to study the reversible insertion of sodium into hard-carbon host structures at room temp.  In this paper we compare these results with those for lithium insertion in the same materials and demonstrate the presence of similar alkali metal insertion mechanisms in both cases.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium Storage Mechanism Investigations through Structural Changes in Hard Carbons</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Alptekin, Hande; Au, Heather; Jensen, Anders CS; Olsson, Emilia; Goktas, Mustafa; Headen, Thomas F.; Adelhelm, Philipp; Cai, Qiong; Drew, Alan J.; Titirici, Maria-Magdalena</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbons, due to their relatively low cost and good electrochem. performance, are considered the most promising anode materials for Na-ion batteries.  Despite the many reported structures of hard carbon, the practical use of hard carbon anodes is largely limited by low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), and the sodium storage mechanism still remains elusive.  A better understanding of the sodium-ion behavior in hard carbon anodes is crucial to develop more efficient sodium-ion batteries.  Here, a series of hard carbon materials with tailored morphol. and surface functionality was synthesized via hydrothermal carbonization and subsequent pyrolysis from 1000 to 1900°C.  Electrochem. results revealed different sodiation-desodiation trends in the galvanostatic potential profiles and varying ICE and were compared with theor. studies to understand the effect of the varying hard carbon structure on the sodium storage process at different voltages.  Furthermore, electrode expansion during cycling was investigated by in situ dilatometry; to the best of our knowledge, this is the first time that the technique has been applied to hard carbons for ion storage mechanism investigation in Na-ion batteries.  Combining exptl. and theor. results, we propose a model for sodium storage in our hard carbons that consist of Na-ion storage at defect sites and by intercalation in the high voltage slope region and via pore filling in the low voltage plateau region; these findings are important for the design of future electrode materials with high capacity and efficiency.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrUrPRzGczXV7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixAtt8EymWUQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhvVCrur7P&amp;md5=43322fa8a9380dfa1f06a6392a094e0f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1016/j.micromeso.2020.110542"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cuesta, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cameán, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Arenillas, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">García, A.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Exploring the application of carbon xerogels as anodes for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cuesta, Nuria; Camean, Ignacio; Arenillas, Ana; Garcia, Ana B.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Microporous and Mesoporous Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">308</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">110542</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">MIMMFJ</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Carbon xerogels (CXs) with the same chem. compn. and BET surface area but different pore sizes (10-200 nm), which had been easily produced in large amts. via a cost-effective microwave-based process, are investigated as anodes for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  The role of textural properties of CXs in the process of sodium ions storage was evaluated.  The most suitable anode for SIBs was CX-100 with a pore size of 100 nm, the largest micropore vol. and the lowest external surface area (Sext), which gives an idea of the most accessible surface of the material, along with relatively high open porosity.  Larger pore sizes facilitate electrolyte penetration, thus improving Na+ ions diffusion inside the electrode, while microporosity is crucial in increasing electrode capacity since Na+ ions storage on CXs is mainly due to absorption on the surface and in structural defects (i.e., microporosity).  Moreover, lowering Sext leads to a decrease in the Na+ ions used in the formation of the SEI layer and irreversibly absorbed during initial cycles, therefore improving electrode performance.  In summary, an optimal combination of textural properties, including pore structure and Sext, should be considered in order to effectively design CXs for SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr9ktubO3c7W7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgvEb4FJLG65Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhs1OksbzK&amp;md5=59cd303e8b1ad296c6ffa3d062466abe</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2020.128104"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, Q.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tuning microstructures of hard carbon for high capacity and rate sodium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin, Qianzheng; Wang, Kangli; Li, Haomiao; Li, Wei; Feng, Pingyuan; Zhang, Zhuchan; Wang, Wei; Zhou, Min; Jiang, Kai</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">417</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">128104</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMEJAJ</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon materials have been attracting great attention as anode of sodium ion batteries (SIBs) owing to their low cost, abundance and high Na-storage capacity.  However, the correlation between microstructures and electrochem. behaviors of hard carbon is still ambiguous, which hinders the rational design and tailor of structures and morphologies of hard carbon for high Na-storage capacities and rates.  Herein, a series of hard carbon near spheres (HCNSs) are designed and prepd. at different carbonization conditions, and the effects of their microstructural evolution on Na-storage behaviors are comprehensively investigated.  The results demonstrate that plateau region in discharge curve of hard carbon is ascribed to Na+ insertion into the long-range ordered carbon structure, while the corresponding slope region is related to the Na+ adsorption on the surface.  Improving ordering degree with suitable interlayer distance (&gt;0.364 nm) can both enhance the "plateau capacity" and total Na-storage capacity.  Meanwhile, enlarging the surface area is able to effectively enhance the "adsorption capacity" at high potential.  The optimized product delivers an excellent reversible capacity of 305 mAh g-1 including a "plateau capacity" of 170 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 0.02 A g-1 and keeps 210 mAh g-1 for 1100 cycles at 1 A g-1.  This work provides a theor. guidance for rationally designing and controlling the microstructures of hard carbon for high-performance SIBs anode.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo11lEfPMyrGLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgvEb4FJLG65Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXktFylsQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=6f5951889b3635371be1ff2b988a5bb2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Johnson, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kudo, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Effect of Local Atomic Structure on Sodium Ion Storage in Hard Amorphous Carbon</span>. <i>Nano Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>21</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">15</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">6504</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">6510</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Effect of Local Atomic Structure on Sodium Ion Storage in Hard Amorphous Carbon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Jiuhui; Johnson, Isaac; Lu, Zhen; Kudo, Akira; Chen, Mingwei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">21</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">15</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6504-6510</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The fundamental understanding of sodium storage mechanisms in amorphous carbon is essential to develop high-performance anode materials for sodium-ion batteries.  However, the intrinsic relation between the structure of amorphous carbon and Na+ storage remains to be debated due to the difficulty in controlling and characterizing the local at. configurations of amorphous carbon.  Here we report quant. measurements of Na+ storage in a low-temp. dealloyed hard carbon with a tunable local structure from completely disordered micropores to gradually increased graphitic order domains.  The structure-capacity-potential correlation not only verifies the disputing "adsorption-intercalation" mechanisms, i.e., Na+ intercalation into local graphitic domains for the low-voltage plateaus and adsorption in fully disordered carbon for the sloping voltage profiles, but also unveils a new mechanism of Na+ adsorption on defective sites of graphitic carbon in the medium-potential sloping region.
